SE News
HD Fraudster fed on the goodwill of old friends
BY Dearbhail McDonald
WC 346 words
PD 19 June 2014
SN Irish Independent
SC IINM
ED 3; National
PG 14
LA English
CY (c) 2014 Independent Newspapers Ireland Ltd

LP 

BREIFNE O'Brien's fraudulent Ponzi scheme, which centred on bogus property deals, fed on the goodwill of some of his oldest and trusted friends. They included Evan Newell, O'Brien's friend and former business mentor who was one of the last investors to get sucked into his investment scheme.

At the height of the property boom, Mr Newell invested in what he thought was an option to buy a shopping centre in Hamburg.

TD 

After a lengthy series of court battles to halt his trial, O'Brien yesterday admitted stealing 2.1m from Evan Newell between June 8 and 15, 2007 at National Irish Bank, Stillorgan, Co Dublin.

Friendship Daniel Maher, also known as Don, had known Breifne O'Brien for 25 years when their friendship, along with his money, evaporated.

Weeks before Mr O'Brien's scheme collapsed, he gave a "half bar" - 500,000 - to O'Brien for a purported property deal in Paris.

Mr Maher was due to get a 10pc return within two months for the investment.

But there was no Paris option.

Louis and Robert Dowley also invested with O'Brien.

Ten years ago Louis Dowley was indebted to O'Brien for cutting him in on what would be his (Dowley's) maiden deal.

This was when O'Brien offered him the opportunity to "buy out" a co-investor on a deal to secure an option on a shopping centre in the UK.

A grateful Dowley was told his lucky break came about because the co-investor had an alleged conflict of interest.

Despite some troubles with the maiden deal - the profits on which were delayed - Louis Dowley and his brother Robert continued to invest in separate deals with O'Brien.

They paid out handsomely to the tax man in relation to their purported profits, but their money had never been used for a legitimate deal.

Yesterday, O'Brien admitted stealing some 4m from Louis Dowley.

Other victims in the sample counts are Martin O'Brien and Pat Doyle.

HD Banks are storing 10,000 unclaimed valuable items
BY Mark Tighe
WC 739 words
PD 21 June 2014
ET 20:01
SN sundaytimes.co.uk
SC SUNDTI
LA English
CY  2014 Times Newspapers Ltd. All rights reserved

LP 

Former Bank of Ireland official says the state should lay claim to treasures with untraceable owners, writes Mark Tighe

IRISH banks are holding more than 10,000 items that were deposited by owners who have either died or forgotten about their valuables, according to a former bank official.

TD 

Jim Connolly claims some of the items in storage could be priceless, and has called on the state to take possession of treasures that cannot be given back to owners or their estates.

Only one bank, Danske Bank Ireland, would reveal how many items of safekeeping it holds. The Danish firm, which took over the former National Irish Bank branch network, said it has catalogued circa 2,000 safe custody items that had been deposited in branches but were now stored centrally in Dublin.

Ulster Bank, Bank of Ireland and AIB, which have the largest branch networks, declined to reveal how many items of value they had in storage. Banking sources have speculated that the number stored by the three biggest banks could be multiples of what Danske Bank has amassed.

Connolly believes there are tens of thousands of items stored in bank vaults, including paintings, jewellery, historical documents and family heirlooms.

In 2005, as part of research for a TV programme, he was invited into Bank of Irelands vault in College Green in Dublin. He said he was shown a trunk deposited from 1903 by a spinster, which had stayed sealed until 1995 when bank staff were ordered to open it by Bill Clintons Secret Service detail before the former American president delivered a speech on the plaza outside. Connolly was told the chest contained ceremonial swords, and a loaded pistol that had caught a sniffer dogs attention.

On May 6, Bank of Ireland told its customers they had 30 days to remove their valuable and legal documents from its vaults as it was phasing out its free safekeeping facility. The bank said the volume of items in safekeeping is causing an unacceptable health, safety and security risk.

Connolly said the thousands of envelopes, boxes, safes, chests, parcels and trunks are deteriorating in vaults and bank safes around the country. He accused the banks of ignoring the history, the wealth, the confessions, the title deeds, the art, the literature and answers that potentially exist in unopened containers. He said as the banks had failed to reunite the items with their owners, it was up to the state to intervene.

I am surprised that banks dont capitalise on the fact that this is one of the few good-news stories for them theyve done their job well in keeping this property safe and its about time they reunited this property with their rightful owners, said Connolly. Much of this property is over 100 years old and hard-pressed citizens could benefit from unexpected windfalls.

The former official with Bank of Ireland said the government may be required to change legislation to ensure it can lay claim to items whose owners cannot be traced. The minister for social protection [Joan Burton] needs to step in and take ownership of these assets on behalf of the state, said Connolly. A simple change to the definition of dormant deposit in our legislation is all that is required.

Connolly met Jimmy Deenihan, the arts and heritage minister, in 2011 to propose the state take possession of unclaimed items of national and historical interest. The minister has not acted on the suggestion.

Bank of Ireland said it was precluded from opening items to assess their contents owing to the terms under which the property is held. However, the bank will be seeking, at a future date, to attempt to contact the owners of unclaimed items, it said. In respect of deceased estates, executors can lay claims to the contents.

Danske Bank said it will continue to hold its 2,000 items, and customers continue to collect them. AIB said it will continue to hold unclaimed items and will respond to any requests from solicitors or executors seeking them.

Ulster Bank said: We continue to hold the items and keep a register which is audited on an annual basis. We can quantify the amount of property, but do not disclose this information. We are confident we satisfy all requirements relating to this issue. Permanent TSB did not respond to queries.

HD Plenty for NAB to do before UK exit
BY James Chessell
WC 1404 words
PD 30 June 2014
SN The Australian Financial Review
SC AFNR
ED First
PG 48
LA English
CY Copyright 2014. Fairfax Media Management Pty Limited.

LP 

Chanticleer

Don't be fooled by the good news in the United Kingdom. National Australia Bank still has plenty of work to do if it wants to exit the region on its own terms and anyone suggesting otherwise is overly optimistic about the strength of the British banking revival.

TD 

As recently as seven years ago in the good ol' days before the global financial crisis it was still fashionable among some commentators and analysts to argue NAB's two-decade attempt to build a substantial British franchise was a cause worth pursuing. Sure it may have sold two Irish banks in 2004 (Northern Bank and National Irish Bank) and explored walking away from the UK altogether but all NAB needed to do to make it a major player in the Old Dart was one more acquisition. Former chief executives Don Argus and Frank Cicutto almost pulled off ambitious deals. Maybe John Stewart would be the man. Or at least that was the theory.

These days it is a truth universally acknowledged that NAB must flog its remaining UK assets the Clydesdale and Yorkshire banks it acquired in 1987 and 1990 respectively as soon as is humanly possible. The units shaved 3 percentage points off NAB's return on equity last year and no amount of improvement will generate decent enough returns to satisfy long-suffering investors.

The problem is NAB hasn't pulled off a deal. The GFC struck and nobody has been willing to pay close to Clydesdale and Yorkshire's 2.7 billion ($4.6 billion) book value - which happens to be the price soon-to-be-departed boss Cameron Clyne wanted.

Not that NAB hasn't tried. Despite the "we are not going to rush" rhetoric under Clyne, NAB and its advisers (Goldman Sachs and, more recently, Morgan Stanley) have approached every man and his dog about a potential deal.

It is understood the closest NAB got was a sale to Spanish bank Santander in early 2013 but disagreements over value as well as the tough UK regulatory environment a factor that is often underestimated in Australia scuppered any chance of a transaction. New breed of bank is emerging

"The UK is clearly a problem asset, that's no question and it's been something I've obviously been evaluating since I took over," Clyne said in early 2013. "But there have been very, very limited options we could have undertaken."

Which brings us to the present day where those pushing for a permanent UK withdrawal for NAB are once again getting their hopes up. The reasons for their optimism are obvious enough. The British economy has picked up to the extent that Bank of England governor Mark Carney is saying interest rates should rise. At the same time a new breed of challenger banks is emerging from the wreckage of the GFC. Former Westpac Banking Corporation boss David Morgan now running US financial services investor JC Flowers in Europe and Asia got the ball rolling in early June with the 413-million float of OneSavings Bank.

Created from what remained of the Kent Reliance Building Society, OneSavings was the first bank to list on the London Stock Exchange in more than a decade. Its peers include Metro Bank and Virgin Money, which are expected to list by 2016, and Lloyds offshoot TSB, which enjoyed a strong sharemarket debut just over a week ago. TSB's performance was exciting for NAB shareholders. Not only is it a similar size to Clydesdale and Yorkshire (27 billion and 38 billion in total assets respectively, according to Bank of America Merrill Lynch), but its listing shows investor appetite for second tier banks. Having failed to sell its UK banks, NAB has done a decent job improving performance under Clyne, who served as chairman of NAB Group Europe since 2012.

Profit margins for Clydesdale are finally expanding with mortgage demand growing with earnings for the six months to March 2014 at 89 million compared with 44 million for the same period in the previous year. Much of Clydesdale's underperformance has been sheeted back to its exposure to commercial property outside London but NAB has solved the problem by transferring about 3.5 billion worth of loans to the Australian parent.

These loans are being sold by Morgan Stanley in tranches as part of "project chestnut". First-round bids were due last week and a number of hedge funds are said to be lining up to pay a decent price due, in part, to growing optimism about the British economy. The positive market conditions are underlined by the speed in which NAB has run down its commercial property book given it was 5.8 billion 18 months ago.Clydesdale remains strong presence

Clydesdale, in particular, remains a good franchise in the north of the UK with its strong presence in the small-to-medium business market and should benefit if the recovery extends beyond London.

Like Australia, the UK has a "big four" banking oligarchy (Lloyds, HSBC, RBS and Barclays) although there is genuine competition in Britain. The warm reception TSB's listing received is seen as a vote of confidence that these challengers can make an impact.

All of this sounds promising for NAB shareholders. Yet on closer inspection, the supposed similarities between TSB and the Australian bank's UK operations don't stack up. TSB floated with a pristine balance sheet, yet Clydesdale is not without its problems. BoA Merrill Lynch's Andrew Hill believes TSB's return on risk-weighted assets a measure that allegedly shows how a bank manages its balance sheet as well as its profitability are twice that of NAB's UK operations. He argues TSB has much stronger capital ratios and even more importantly none of the pension liabilities or conduct fines Clydesdale faces.

Then there is the underlying business. As UBS banking analyst Jonathan Mott believes the small-to-medium business is "decaying" having shrunk by from 10.9 billion to 8.3 billion over the past 18 months a faster decline rate than the commercial real estate portfolio in absolute terms. An 8-billion book is less attractive to potential acquirers, meaning they are less likely to pay close to book value for the entire UK business. The key question for many mid-sized "challenger" banks such as NAB UK and TSB is where the growth is going to come from. They are not small and nimble enough to take risks yet not big enough to influence the market. The bottom line is Lloyds was only able to get around 0.8 times book value. There is little chance the NAB board will do better with a lesser asset.New bosses like to clear the decks

Clyne's replacement, Andrew Thorburn, takes over the top job on August 1. It is not uncommon for new chief executives to "clear the decks" by writing down troublesome assets and announcing strategic reviews that lead to divestments. Thorburn's problem is that the strategic review has been done and there are no buyers at a price NAB would find acceptable. It is believed NAB even looked at bidding for TSB before the float in an attempt to create a business with sufficient scale to compete with the big four. But it was not able to come up with an offer.

Thorburn would find it difficult to overturn a strategy based around selling the UK unit at book value given it has been backed by chairman Michael Chaney, who has served on board for almost 10 years.

But the danger is NAB becomes "trapped" in the UK, to use Mott's word, as its relative attractiveness to potential acquirers declines. This scenario is made worse by NAB having to transfer capital to the UK to satisfy UK regulatory requirements. The Australian bank is unlikely to get this money back in the event of a sale. Consolidation in the middle part of the UK banking system is likely - and the TSB float is a promising sign - but valuations have not improved enough to generate bumper prices.

A source of frustration for investors for the best part of a decade, NAB's misadventures in Britain are unlikely to be solved anytime soon unless the board is willing to cop a write-down.

j.chessell@afr.com.au Twitter: @jameschessell

HD *S&P Afrms 'AAA' Rtgs On Danske Bank's Cover Pool C
WC 1829 words
PD 30 June 2014
ET 10:47
SN Dow Jones Institutional News
SC DJDN
LA English
CY Copyright 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

LP 

30 Jun 2014 10:47 ET Press Release: S&P Afrms 'AAA' Rtgs On Danske Bank's Cover Pool C

TD 

 
 
The following is a press release from Standard & Poor's: 
 
OVERVIEW 
 
     -- We have reviewed the performance of the covered bonds secured under 
Danske Bank A/S' cover pool C. 
     -- Following our review, we are affirming our 'AAA' ratings on the 
covered bonds secured under Danske Bank A/S' cover pool C. 
     -- The stable outlook reflects our view that the program's 
characteristics would allow us to maintain our 'AAA' ratings on the covered 
bonds, even if we were to lower our long-term issuer credit rating on Danske 
Bank by two notches, or if we were to revise the program's asset-liability 
mismatch classification to "high". 
     -- Our ratings are based on our applicable criteria. However, please note 
that certain criteria are under review. Until such time that we adopt new 
criteria, we will continue to rate and surveil these covered bonds using our 
existing criteria. 
 
FRANKFURT (Standard & Poor's) June 30, 2014--Standard & Poor's Ratings 
Services today affirmed its 'AAA' credit ratings on the covered bonds secured 
under cover pool C, issued under Danske Bank A/S' EUR30 billion global covered 
bond program. The outlook is stable. 
 
Under our asset-liability mismatch (ALMM) criteria, we arrive at the rating by 
determining the maximum potential number of notches of uplift on the long-term 
'A' issuer credit rating (ICR) on Danske Bank (see "Revised Methodology And 
Assumptions For Assessing Asset-Liability Mismatch Risk In Covered Bonds," 
published on Dec. 16, 2009). We classify the program's ALMM risk as "low" and 
have assigned it to category '1' under our criteria. This means that we 
consider the program to be able to raise funds, either through asset sales or 
by borrowing. Among other factors, category '1' programs are also issued in 
markets that we view as having long, well-established histories. The 
combination of these features allows a maximum seven-notch uplift from our 
long-term 'A' ICR on Danske Bank. 
 
The 30.68% available credit enhancement as of May 2014 is above the target 
credit enhancement of 13.25% for the covered bonds to achieve a 'AAA' rating. 
The target credit enhancement is a product of Standard & Poor's credit and 
market risk stresses. We have therefore affirmed our 'AAA' rating on the 
program. 
 
The stable outlook reflects our opinion that adverse movements in the ICR on 
Danske Bank or the ALMM measure would not automatically result in a change to 
our ratings on the covered bonds. The program could achieve seven notches of 
uplift, but only needs five to reach a 'AAA' rating, resulting in a buffer of 
two unused notches of uplift. Therefore, all else being equal, the program 
could still maintain our currently assigned 'AAA' rating if we were to lower 
our long-term ICR on Danske Bank by two notches, or if we were to revise our 
ALMM classification to "high" from "low". 
 
POTENTIAL EFFECTS OF PROPOSED CRITERIA CHANGES 
 
Our ratings are based on our applicable criteria, including those set out in 
the criteria articles "Revised Methodology And Assumptions For Assessing 
Asset-Liability Mismatch Risk In Covered Bonds," published on Dec. 16, 2009, 
"Nonsovereign Ratings That Exceed EMU Sovereign Ratings: Methodology And 
Assumptions," published June 14, 2011 and "Methodology And Assumptions: Update 
To The Criteria For Rating Italian Residential Mortgage-Backed Securities," 
published Jan. 6, 2009. However, please note that these criteria are under 
review (see "S&P Publishes Advance Notice of Proposed Criteria Change For 
Covered Bonds," published on April 29, 2014 and "Request For Comment: 
Methodology And Assumptions For Ratings Above The Sovereign-Single 
Jurisdiction Structured Finance," published Oct. 14, 2013. 
 
As a result of this review, our future criteria applicable to rating covered 
bonds may differ from our current criteria. These criteria changes may affect 
the ratings on the outstanding covered bonds. Until such time that we adopt 
new criteria, we will continue to rate and surveil these covered bonds using 
our existing criteria (see the list of related criteria and research below). 
 
RELATED CRITERIA AND RESEARCH 
 
Related Criteria

Finance, Sept. 13, 2013 
     -- Counterparty Risk Framework Methodology And Assumptions, June 25, 2013 
     -- Covered Bond Ratings Framework: Methodology And Assumptions, June 26, 
2012 
     -- Nonsovereign Ratings That Exceed EMU Sovereign Ratings: Methodology 
And Assumptions, June 14, 2011 
     -- Principles Of Credit Ratings, Feb. 16, 2011 
     -- Methodology: Credit Stability Criteria, May 3, 2010 
     -- Revised Methodology And Assumptions For Assessing Asset-Liability 
Mismatch Risk In Covered Bonds, Dec. 16, 2009 
     -- Methodology And Assumptions: Update To The Criteria For Rating Italian 
Residential Mortgage-Backed Securities, Jan. 9, 2009 
     -- Criteria For Rating Swedish Covered Bonds, June 20, 2006 
     -- Cash Flow Criteria for European RMBS Transactions, Nov. 20, 2003 
 
Related Research

Asset Quality; Outlook Negative, April 29, 2014 
     -- Advance Notice Of Proposed Criteria Change for Covered Bonds, April 
29, 2014 
     -- Credit FAQ: What Factors Do We Consider When Analyzing Commingling And

HD Success After Bailouts
BY By Emese Bartha
WC 536 words
PD 3 July 2014
SN The Wall Street Journal Europe
SC WSJE
PG 20
LA English
CY (Copyright (c) 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

CX 

Corrections & Amplifications

Portugal launched a $4.5 billion, 10-year U.S. dollar-denominated bond via a syndicate of banks Wednesday. A Business & Finance story Thursday about borrowing by Portugal and Ireland incorrectly said the figure was $4 billion.

(WSJE July 4, 2014)

(END)

LP 

Portugal and Ireland, countries that have weaned themselves off international financial aid, are making new forays into capital markets this week.

Portugal offered a $4 billion, 10-year U.S. dollar-denominated bond via a syndicate of banks on Wednesday, in a fresh sign of smooth and steady market access, following its recent exit from a three-year bailout program.

TD 

The total order book exceeded $10 billion for the October 2024-dated bond. The initial price guidance of 2.65 percentage points above Treasurys was tightened to 2.60 percentage points, one of the lead managers said. The lead managers on the transaction were Barclays PLC, Danske Bank AS, HSBC Holdings PLC and Societe Generale CIB.

Joao Moreira Rato, president of Portugal's debt agency, IGCP, said in an interview last week that the country was considering selling dollar-denominated bonds for the first time in four years. Mr. Rato said the issuance in dollars was aimed at diversifying Portugal's investor base.

Portugal exited its 78 billion euro ($106.7 billion) three-year bailout in May without asking for a precautionary credit line, and ratings firms have praised the country's achievements in fiscal consolidation. On May 9, Moody's Investors Service Inc. raised Portugal's rating to Ba2, or two levels into junk from three levels, citing improved economic and market conditions. On the same day, Standard & Poor's Ratings Services revised Portugal's outlook to stable from negative, and kept the country's rating at BB, also two levels into junk.

Ireland, meanwhile, which left its 67.5 billion euro bailout last December, reduced the amount of redemptions it will have to pay back to investors in 2016 by slightly more than 2 billion euros through two operations -- an outright buyback of its 4.60% April 2016 bond, and an exchange of the same 2016-dated bond for the 3.90% 2023 bond. The exchange operation was done on a 2-for-1 nominal basis -- one 2023-dated bond sold for two 2016-dated bonds bought back.

As a result of the moves, 2.037 billion euros of the 2016-dated bond has been canceled, which in turn reduces its amount outstanding to 8.132 billion euros from 10.169 billion euros, Ireland's National Treasury Management Agency said.

Portuguese and Irish government bonds are in demand as investors are attracted by their relatively high yields compared with the euro zone's record-low interest rates.

Portugal's 10-year euro-denominated bond yield is trading at around 3.62%, compared with yields of around 6% at the beginning of the year. Irish 10-year bonds are trading at a 2.40% yield, down from around 3.42% at the beginning of the year. By comparison, 10-year German Bunds, Europe's benchmark, are trading at a yield of around 1.29%.

HD Portugal, Ireland Make New Forays Into Capital Markets; Investors Attracted by Higher Bond Yields Amid Low Interest Environment
BY By Emese Bartha
WC 579 words
PD 3 July 2014
ET 09:40
SN The Wall Street Journal Online
SC WSJO
ED WSJ.com
LA English
CY Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

Portugal and Ireland, countries that have weaned themselves off international financial aid, are making new forays into capital markets this week.

Portugal offered a $4.5 billion, 10-year U.S. dollar-denominated bond via a syndicate of banks Wednesday, in a new sign of smooth and steady market access, following its recent exit from a three-year bailout program.

TD 

This has been the largest U.S. dollar benchmark from a sovereign in the 10-years' segment, and also the largest U.S. dollar sovereign benchmark since Germany's $5 billion issue in 2005, the lead managers said. The Portuguese bond was priced at 99.221 to yield 5.225%, they added.

The total order book exceeded $10 billion for the October 2024-dated bond. The initial price guidance of 2.65 percentage points above Treasurys was tightened to 2.60 percentage points, one of the lead managers said. The lead managers on the transaction were Barclays PLC, Danske Bank AS, HSBC Holdings PLC and Socit Gnrale CIB.

Joao Moreira Rato, president of Portugal's debt agency, IGCP, said in an interview last week that the country was considering selling dollar-denominated bonds for the first time in four years. Mr. Rato said the issuance in dollars was aimed at diversifying Portugal's investor base.

Portugal exited its 78 billion ($106.7 billion) three-year bailout in May without asking for a precautionary credit line, and ratings firms have praised the country's achievements in fiscal consolidation. On May 9, Moody's Investors Service Inc. raised Portugal's rating to Ba2, or two levels into junk from three levels, citing improved economic and market conditions. On the same day, Standard & Poor's Ratings Services revised Portugal's outlook to stable from negative, and kept the country's rating at BB, also two levels into junk.

Ireland, meanwhile, which left its 67.5 billion bailout last December, reduced the amount of redemptions it will have to pay back to investors in 2016 by slightly more than 2 billion through two operationsan outright buyback of its 4.60% April 2016 bond, and an exchange of the same 2016-dated bond for the 3.90% 2023 bond. The exchange operation was done on a 2-for-1 nominal basisone 2023-dated bond sold for two 2016-dated bonds bought back.

As a result of the moves, 2.037 billion of the 2016-dated bond has been canceled, which in turn reduces its amount outstanding to 8.132 billion from 10.169 billion, Ireland's National Treasury Management Agency said.

Portuguese and Irish government bonds are in demand as investors are attracted by their relatively high yields compared with the euro zone's record-low interest rates.

Portugal's 10-year euro-denominated bond yield is trading at around 3.62%, compared with yields of around 6% at the beginning of the year. Irish 10-year bonds are trading at a 2.40% yield, down from around 3.42% at the beginning of the year. By comparison, 10-year German Bunds, Europe's benchmark, are trading at a yield of around 1.29%.

Corrections & Amplifications

Portugal offered a $4.5 billion, 10-year U.S. dollar-denominated bond via a syndicate of banks Wednesday. An earlier version of this article incorrectly reported the figure at $4 billion.

HD Ex-minister denies bankruptcy
BY Stephen OBrien Political Editor
WC 348 words
PD 19 July 2014
ET 20:01
SN sundaytimes.co.uk
SC SUNDTI
LA English
CY  2014 Times Newspapers Ltd. All rights reserved

LP 

JOHN PERRY, the former junior minister for small business, has dismissed reports that an overseas bank is about to pursue him for bankruptcy, and said he has fully honoured a court agreement over a 2.5m debt.

Perry, a Sligo-North Leitrim TD demoted by Enda Kenny last week, spoke for the first time about his arrangement with Danske Bank over a 2.47m debt and said the judgment was being honoured to the satisfaction of both parties.

TD 

The judgment is to be registered in Stubbs Gazette this week, but he said this did not signify any change in Danske Banks position.

The bank, in due course, will be notifying that this judgment has been satisfied, which will be the case, Perry said yesterday.

Any suggestion of imminent bankruptcy proceedings was completely untrue, he said, after one newspaper reported that he faced the prospect of bankruptcy on foot of the Stubbs Gazette listing.

Absolutely not, they are categorically assuring us. none whatever, he said. There is an agreement that will be fully honoured and that will be very obvious in due course.

Perrys lawyer, Paul Tweed, issued a statement on his behalf. I have clear and categoric instructions that the agreement my client has and continues to have with Danske Bank is being concluded to the mutual satisfaction of both parties and that any registered judgment forms part of that agreement.

Perry said: This matter is resolved and that registration is just part of the agreement. All I can say is that that is part of the agreement that was agreed last year. That agreement we have entered will be fully honoured.

Danske Bank declined to comment.

Danske issued proceedings against Perry and his wife Marie in October 2011 in relation to a loan secured against the familys businesses Stone Park restaurant and Perrys Hardware in Ballymote, Co Sligo, and 50 acres of agricultural land near Ballymote.

In July of last year, the bank secured a judgment against them for 2.47m.

HD Danske Bank Q2 result expected to be the best since 2008
WC 492 words
PD 21 July 2014
ET 07:18
SN Reuters News
SC LBA
LA English
CY (c) 2014 Reuters Limited

LP 

COPENHAGEN, July 21 (Reuters) - Denmark's biggest financial institution Danske Bank is expected to report its best quarterly result since the global financial crisis began in 2008, boosted by a one-off item from the sale of a stake in payment services company Nets Holding.

Danske Bank's share price has outperformed Nordic peers so far his year and analysts expect more to come after a strong quarterly result on Thursday.

TD 

"I see opportunity for an upgrade of the full-year guidance," Sydbank analyst Bjorn Schwarz said. Like other analysts, he expects management to continue to bring down loan losses and cut costs.

Danske Bank, the biggest Nordic financial institution by market capitalisation a decade ago but now lying in sixth place, was hit hard by a property market collapse in Ireland, where it bought a bank before the crisis, and by a sluggish Danish economy.

It is now expected to report pretax profit of 4.87 billion Danish crowns ($885 million) and a net profit of 3.69 billion crowns according to a Reuters poll. Even without the one-off of around 1 billion crowns, the pretax profit would be the best since 2008.

The quarterly results would have been helped by money saved on interest payments after Danske Bank repaid a 24 billion crown state loan in April.

The bank began recovering since early 2013 driven by lower loan losses. Analysts are now looking for cost-cutting programmes.

"Business review news about new cost-saving initiatives or the divestment of less profitable business units could fuel a positive share price reaction," analyst Christian Hede from Nordea wrote in a note to clients.

Danske Bank is the lowest valued stock among the biggest Nordic banks with a price-to-book ratio of 1.07. Handelsbanken is the highest valued with a price-to-book ratio of 1.84, Swedbank is third with 1.80, SEB with 1.63, Nordea with 1.39 while Norwegian DNB is second lowest with 1.30.

Media report have suggested that Danske Bank wants to sell its Swedish unit but Chief Executive Thomas Borgen told Reuters in May that the bank was fully committed to Norway and Sweden.

Analysts, however, are still looking at the Swedish business due to strong competition there.

"We expect management to explain further about the strategy for Sweden - as the current market share of 5 percent for the Swedish Retail Banking unit does not provide sufficient economies of scale," analyst Thomas Eskildsen from Jyske Bank wrote in a note.

Share prices for Danish lenders have gone up 23 percent so far this year, strongly outperforming their five Nordic peers which are between 2.1 and 7.4 percent higher. Shares in Swedbank are down 2.9 percent for the year.

($1 = 5.5092 Danish crowns) (Reporting by Ole Mikkelsen Editing by Jeremy Gaunt)

HD UPDATE 1-Denmark accuses Danske Bank unit of insider information leak
WC 207 words
PD 21 July 2014
ET 12:23
SN Reuters News
SC LBA
LA English
CY (c) 2014 Reuters Limited

LP 

(Adds background on previous charges, how the case was uncovered)

TD 

COPENHAGEN, July 21 (Reuters) - Denmark's public prosecutor for serious economic and international crime has brought an additional accusation against Danske Bank's mortgage unit Realkredit Danmark and one of its employees, the bank said on Monday.

It said the new allegation concerns insider information on a type of Danish adjustable mortgage loan called Flexlaan.

An employee at the mortgage unit is accused of giving insider information to an employee at Danske Bank's market division in August 2009 about a new mortgage bond, which was to be issued a few days later by Realkredit Danmark, the bank said.

Danske Bank said the new accusation concerns the same group of people that the prosecutor earlier this year said it was investigating over cases of alleged price manipulation in 2009.

Analysts have said they did not expect Danske Bank to take a big financial hit from the previous allegations, although its reputation could suffer.

Danske Bank said it had uncovered the new case in an internal investigation conducted at its own initiative and passed the results on to the authorities.

HD Danske Bank Raises 2014 Outlook as Second-Quarter Profit Exceeds Expectations
BY By Juhana Rossi
WC 441 words
PD 24 July 2014
ET 03:15
SN Dow Jones Institutional News
SC DJDN
LA English
CY Copyright 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

LP 

Danish lender Danske Bank A/S (DANSKE.KO) Thursday bumped up its profit expectations for 2014, after its net profit nearly doubled in the second quarter on the back of narrowing loan losses and a larger-than-expected rise in net interest income.

Danske said it now expects its annual net profit to be in the range of 10 billion Danish kroner to 13 billion Danish kroner ($1.81 billion to $2.35 billion) in 2014. It had projected a net profit at the higher end of the range of DKK9 billion to DKK12 billion in its previous guidance issued in May.

TD 

"Expenses are declining as expected, the positive trend in impairments is continuing, and we saw growing customer activity in the second quarter of 2014," Chief Executive Thomas F. Borgen said in a statement.

The lender, which is Denmark's biggest bank by market capitalization, reported a net profit of DKK4.05 billion for the three months to June 30, up 86% from DKK2.18 billion in the same period a year ago and above the median forecast of DKK2.53 billion in an analyst poll by Reuters.

Buoyed by higher customer activity, net interest income rose to DKK5.90 billion in the quarter from DKK5.46 billion in the year-ago period, topping the analysts' forecast of DKK5.78 billion. Loan losses for the period narrowed to DKK626 million from DKK813 million compared with the analysts' forecast of DKK675 million.

While other Nordic banks such as Nordea Bank AB (NDA.SK)--which reported solid quarterly results a week ago--have in recent years stood apart from their troubled euro-zone peers by delivering good shareholder returns, Danske Bank was badly hurt in the 2008 financial crisis owing to its exposure to Ireland. It has taken losses there in recent years in the wake of the crisis and is now closing its operations in Ireland.

Danske booked a pre-tax loss of DKK0.8 billion from its non-core or mainly Ireland-linked activities in the June quarter. "The result was in line with our expectations, and the winding-up of our non-core activities is proceeding according to plan," the bank said.

HD Danske Bank raises profit outlook as income rises
WC 303 words
PD 24 July 2014
ET 05:34
SN Agence France Presse
SC AFPR
LA English
CY Copyright Agence France-Presse, 2014 All reproduction and presentation rights reserved.

LP 

Denmark's biggest bank, Danske Bank, on Thursday raised its full-year net profit guidance citing rising income as it posted its strongest quarterly result since the country's housing market collapsed in 2008.

"Our progress thus far in 2014 shows that we are on track to create a more customer-focused, cost-effective and competitive bank," chief executive Thomas Borgen said in a statement.

TD 

The bank expects annual net profit to be between 10 billion and 13 billion kroner (1.3-1.7 billion euros, $1.8-$2.3 billion) rather than a previous estimate at the higher end of a range of 9-12 billion kroner.

Net profit in the three months ending 30 June rose 85 percent to 4.05 billion kroner, driven by a three-percent rise in net interest income, falling loan impairment charges, and a 1.0-billion kroner gain from the sale of the group's stake in payment services provider Nets.

"The stronger outlook is owing mainly to positive developments in expenses and impairments as well as the positive effect relating to Nets," Danske Bank said in a statement.

The group said a pre-tax loss of 800 million kroner from non-core activities -- mainly its operations in Ireland, where it is winding down operations after being hit by a burst property bubble -- was in line with expectations.

"Interest and fee income is clearly better than expected, and shows that the economy is doing better," Sydbank analyst Bjoern Schwarz told news agency Ritzau.

Cost reductions were also "going really well," he added.

Shares in Danske Bank were up by 5.2 percent in late morning trading on the Copenhagen bourse, where the main index was 0.5 percent higher.

HD UPDATE 2-Danske Bank shares hit six-year high after raised outlook
WC 667 words
PD 24 July 2014
ET 04:30
SN Reuters News
SC LBA
LA English
CY (c) 2014 Reuters Limited

LP 

* Q2 pretax 5 bln crowns vs f'cast 4.87 bln

* Sees FY net between 10 bln 13 bln, vs prev f'cast 9-12 bln

TD 

* Aims to increase market share in Sweden and Norway

* Share rise to highest level since May 2008 (Adds analyst comments, CEO comment, share price)

By Ole Mikkelsen

COPENHAGEN, July 24 (Reuters) - Danske Bank A/S has raised its 2014 net profit forecast after posting its best quarterly result since the start of the global financial crisis in 2008, sending its shares up 4.5 percent to a six-year high.

Denmark's largest lender said it now expected to earn between 10 billion Danish crowns ($1.8 billion) and 13 billion in net profit in 2014, against a previous forecast of hitting the higher end of a 9 to 12 billion crown range.

The bank was hit hard by the European financial crisis, especially its exposure to Ireland, the sluggish Danish economy and a collapse in its domestic housing market. Its results have been recovering, but only slowly, since 2013.

Executive Thomas Borgen said Danske Bank would invest in Norway and Sweden where it wants to grow in coming years. Its focus previously had been more on trimming costs rather than expansion and investment.

"We have a 4 percent market share in both Norway and Sweden. We can see that with some investment we can really be a challenger in those markets," Borgen told Reuters. "We will unquestionably be in those markets and develop those businesses further."

Shares in the bank rose by as much as 4.5 percent to 162.50 crowns, their highest since May 2008.

"They are doing well on cutting costs ... Costs were lower than expected and so were loan impairment charges. So the bank can really reap the benefits of its initiatives in this quarter," said Bjorn Schwartz, chief analyst at Sydbank.

"I think we could (see) another upgrade later in the year if the general development continues along the lines we have seen so far this year," Schwartz said, adding that the bank could reach a net result of between 11 and 14 billion crowns.

BETTER THAN EXPECTED

The company posted a pretax profit of 5 billion crowns in the second quarter compared with 2.7 billion in the same quarter a year ago. Net profit amounted to 4 billion crowns against 2.2 billion a year ago.

Analysts had on average forecast a pretax profit of 4.87 billion crowns and a net 3.69 billion.

The latest results were boosted by 1 billion crowns from the sale of a stake in Nets Holding, a payment services company.

Danske Bank was formerly the Nordic region's top financial institution by stock market value, before slipping to sixth place as the financial crisis took its toll.

Its performance began to recover as of 2013, as it trimmed back on loan losses and initiated cost-cutting programmes. Its shares have outperformed Nordic peers so far this year.

In the second quarter of this year, its loan impairment charges amounted to 626 million crowns, less than analysts' expectations of 713 million and the 813 million it booked in charges a year ago.

However Danske is the lowest-valued stock among the biggest Nordic banks, with a price-to-book ratio of 1.08, according to Reuters data.

Handelsbanken is the highest valued with a ratio of 1.87, Swedbank is second with 1.85, SEB has a 1.66 ratio, Nordea a 1.41 ratio while Norwegian DNB is second lowest on 1.29.

Shares in Danish lenders have gained 25 percent so far this year, strongly outperforming their five Nordic peers which are between little changed for Swedbank and just over 9 percent higher for Handelsbanken.

($1 = 5.5411 Danish Crowns) (Additional reporting by Teis Jensen; Writing by Sabina Zawadzki; Editing by Pravin Char and David Holmes)

In 2013, the costs were mainly affected by the implementation of euro.

HD Danske Bank posts best quarterly results since financial crisis
BY By Michael Pooler
WC 473 words
PD 24 July 2014
ET 11:05
SN Financial Times (FT.Com)
SC FTCMA
LA English
CY Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web.

LP 

Danske Bank continued on its path to recovery as it boosted its full-year profit forecast and posted its best quarterly results since the financial crisis.

A combination of narrowing loan losses, a larger than expected rise in net interest income, and cost savings helped drive pre-tax profit at Denmark's biggest lender by market capitalisation to DKr5bn ($900m) in the second quarter, up 85 per cent year on year and beating market expectations.

TD 

The results were boosted by the DKr1bn sale of payment services company Nets Holding, and led Danske to raise its 2014 profit guidance to DKr10bn to DKr13bn, up from the DKr9bn to DKr12bn it forecast in May.

The good news on profits offset what analysts called "disappointing" revenue growth and a fall in income from Danske's market making activities. The Danish bank, like its rivals elsewhere, has been affected by low interest rates and muted volatility across fixed income, currencies and commodities markets.

Ronit Ghose, a Citibank analyst, said: "The only negative has been fixed income trading, which has been an issue through the sector."

Danske was one of the Scandinavian banks worst affected by the financial crisis, Its chief executive, Thomas Borgen, said the second-quarter results vindicated his turnround plans, which focused on cost-cutting.

"We can see the initiatives we have launched around customers and costs are paying off," Mr Borgen said. "We have reduced costs by 6 per cent in the half-year [but] we will remain diligent as we need to stay competitive to our customers."

Danske's after-tax return on equity rose from 2.9 per cent in the first half of 2013 to 4.6 per cent this year, and Mr Borgen said the measures afoot would propel the bank towards its longer-term target of 12 per cent. The bank pleased investors in February by restoring a dividend for the first time since 2007.

Shares in Danske surged 5 per cent in morning trading before settling around DKr163, a six-year high. The stock has gained 30 per cent this year.

However, some analysts questioned the continuation of the recovery.

Berenberg Bank analysts, which gave a sell recommendation, said Danske had benefited from lower funding costs after refinancing expensive hybrid capital raised from the Danish government in 2009.

But they added: "Revenues were disappointing, trading income was weak and they are facing margin pressure and weak appetite for loans."

Danske has reduced the resources of its FICC operations by 20 per cent, but the bank has not decided whether to slash more, Mr Borgen said.

"We are cautious about scaling back further before we can be sure that the changes [in trading conditions] are cyclical or structural. So far we think it is cyclical," he said.

HD  Danske Bank: showing how its done
BY Richard Stovin-Bradford
WC 391 words
PD 24 July 2014
ET 06:46
SN Financial Times (FT.Com)
SC FTCMA
LA English
CY Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web.

LP 

Yet more encouraging results from a Nordic bank.

Danske Banks second quarter net income climbed by 44 per cent from a year ago to DKr4bn, comfortably beating expectations.

TD 

Although, like its Swedish peers reporting earnings last week, the Danish lender managed to report decent revenue growth in its case 16 per cent the DKr11.4bn raked in undershot even punchier expectations.

Those expectations were predicated on Danske reporting a stronger performance in trading always a lumpy revenue line. It climbed by just 2 per cent in the quarter. Just as well that net interest income growth was 8 per cent and fee income increased by 13 per cent. Danskes net interest income benefited from repaying state aid in April.

But, as ever with Scandinavian lenders already in the vanguard of banks that have grasped the need to manage for returns the real standout is cost discipline, which was better than expected. Danske shaved more than 6 per cent, with the main component staff costs falling by 4 per cent as headcount falls, mostly in the personal banking area.

And, like its peers, asset quality is improving. Bad loan charges dropped by almost a quarter from a year earlier and are set to keep falling. Danskes loan loss ratio is just 0.15 per cent, enough to make most southern European bankers weep.

Its return on equity has recovered sharply to 10.2 per cent, off the embarrassingly low base of 6.2 per cent a year ago, despite its capital issues.

Nor does Danskes capital base give cause for concern, with a Basel III ratio of 13.2 per cent, up 20 basis points from the first quarter. Last month it ended its year-long wrangle with Denmarks financial regulator over capital adequacy and is now toeing the line.

The Danish lenders results are further confirmation of the gap in mentality, vision and performance being opened up over their European rivals by the Nordics. Its not as if what they do is rocket science. Check what Danske says: We remained focused on increasing customer satisfaction and improving the return on equity.

And, despite his run-ins with his regulator, CEO Thomas Borgen is one of the few in the European banking business increasing his profit guidance.

SE Business
HD Regulator warns Danske Bank
WC 86 words
PD 5 August 2014
SN i
SC IIND
ED 1ST
PG 41
LA English
CY  2014 Independent Print Ltd. All rights reserved

LP 

Business | From the business pages Copenhagen Daily Post

Denmark's financial regulator has warned Danske Bank to raise underestimated risk weights on consumer loans by 2.7bn kroner (2.9m). Scandinavia's second largest bank has to correct the risk of losses in part of its consumer debt assets, the Financial Supervisory Authority said. It is the second time the bank has been reprimanded since June 2013, when the FSA ordered it to raise assets by as much as 100bn kroner.

HD US stocks rally but mood remains fragile
BY By Dave Shellock
WC 841 words
PD 8 August 2014
ET 16:13
SN Financial Times (FT.Com)
SC FTCMA
LA English
CY Copyright 2014 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web.

LP 

Friday 21:00 BST. US equities rallied strongly at the end of a week that saw geopolitical factors replace concerns about US interest rates as a key driver of risk appetite.

"Economic fundamentals are off the table for a while until we have more clarification on the political developments," said Allan von Mehren, chief analyst at Danske Bank.

TD 

Wall Street managed to overcome an initial bout of nerves that followed President Obama's authorisation of US air strikes against Islamist militants in Iraq.

And the early rise for US stocks picked up pace as reports emerged that Russian military exercises near the Ukrainian border had ended, and troops would return to their bases.

But the mood in the markets remained fragile following this week's sharp ratcheting up of tensions between Moscow and the west over Russia's role in the Ukraine crisis.

European stocks remained under pressure while top-tier government bonds continued to attracted demand as nervous investors sought safety from the volatility that has swept through global markets in the past couple of weeks.

In New York, the S&P 500 index rose 1.2 per cent to 1,931, putting it in positive territory for the week.

However, the US equity benchmark was still down 2.9 per cent from the record closing high reached last month.

Across the Atlantic, the FTSE Eurofirst 300 fell 0.7 per cent for a five-day decline of 2 per cent. The Xetra Dax in Frankfurt pared an early fall to close 0.3 per cent lower, although the index was down 10 per cent from a record intraday peak in June.

That decline reflected worries about Germany's exposure to the Russian economy, particularly after Moscow unveiled import restrictions in response to western sanctions.

"It's important to remember that while flare-ups in Ukraine and Iraq will naturally result in negative kneejerk reactions, a more pronounced correction can only be expected if these events are perceived to provoke a slowdown in global growth," said Adrian Miller, director of fixed income strategy at GMP Securities.

"And while the Ukraine situation has begun to show such ties to economic growth - due in large part to sanctions against Russia and their retaliatory measures - such ties are not evident with the Iraq situation."

Indeed, a sharp early rally for German government bonds - which saw the 10-year Bund yield hit a record low for a third successive session, and the two-year yield briefly turn negative - faded by the close of trade.

The Bund yield ended 2 basis points lower on Friday at 1.05 per cent, having touched 1.02 per cent earlier. That left it 8bp lower over the week.

In a similar vein the 10-year US Treasury yield touched a 13-month trough of 2.35 per cent but was last trading flat at 2.40 per cent, for a weekly drop of 11bp.

Divyang Shah, global strategist at IFR Markets, noted that the drop in the Bund yield in the past three days had been almost as dramatic as the decline seen in May.

"The big difference between then and now is that we have a hefty correction on the Xetra Dax," he said. "This is what makes the latest reduction in Bund yields and correction on peripheral yields more significant.

"It is important to remember equity markets have been leading the charge when it comes to de-risking. This is still an orderly trimming of risk positions as opposed to a dash for the exit, but we have seen in the past that a lack of liquidity/depth can see things escalate."

The steady decline in US Treasury yields this week had relatively little impact on the dollar as the US currency climbed to a 10-month high against a basket of peers.

However, the dollar index was down 0.2 per cent on Friday as the US unit slipped slightly against the yen to Y102.06 and the euro rallied 0.4 per cent to $1.3411.

The single currency had touched a nine-month low versus the dollar after Mario Draghi, president of the European Central Bank, highlighted that eurozone and US monetary policy would remain on a divergent path for some time. Mr Draghi left the door open to further easing as he noted the risks to eurozone growth - already viewed by most as fragile - from rising geopolitical tensions.

Figures this week showed Italy slipping back into recession in the second quarter while hopes that German gross domestic product had held up over the period were undermined by a succession of weak data releases. Italy's 10-year government bond yield rose 8bp to 2.73 per cent over the course of the week.

Gold was down $2 on Friday at $1,310 an ounce, although its traditional role as a bolt-hole for nervous investors was back in play as it rose $17, or 1.3 per cent, over the week.

HD *S&P Affirms Danske Bank Cover Pool D Bonds Rtgs At 'AAA'
WC 1507 words
PD 12 August 2014
ET 05:21
SN Dow Jones Institutional News
SC DJDN
LA English
CY Copyright 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

LP 

12 Aug 2014 05:21 ET Press Release: S&P Affirms Danske Bank Cover Pool D Bonds Rtgs At 'AAA'

TD 

 
 
The following is a press release from Standard & Poor's: 
 
OVERVIEW 
     -- We have reviewed the performance of Danske Bank A/S' cover pool D 
mortgage covered bond program. 
     -- We are affirming our 'AAA' ratings on the covered bond program and all 
series of covered bonds issued under it because the available 
overcollateralization supports the maximum number of notches of uplift from 
the issuer credit rating (ICR). 
     -- The stable outlook reflects our view that adverse changes to either 
the long-term ICR on Danske Bank or the level of asset liability mismatch 
would not automatically trigger a change to the ratings. 
 
RATING ACTION 
LONDON (Standard & Poor's) Aug. 12, 2014--Standard & Poor's Ratings Services 
today affirmed its 'AAA' credit ratings on Danske Bank A/S' cover pool D 
covered bond program and all series of covered bonds issued under it. The 
outlook is stable. 
 
Today's affirmation follows our review of information from Danske Bank as of 
March 31, 2014. 
 
We classify the program's asset liability mismatch (ALMM) risk as "low" and we 
have assigned the program to Category 1 of our ALMM criteria (see "Revised 
Methodology And Assumptions For Assessing Asset-Liability Mismatch Risk In 
Covered Bonds," published on Dec. 16, 2009 on RatingsDirect). Our ALMM 
criteria therefore allow a maximum uplift of seven notches from our long-term 
'A' issuer credit rating (ICR) on Danske Bank A/S. The cover pool consists 
entirely of mortgages originated in Denmark. The available 
overcollateralization of 23.38% is sufficient to cover the target credit 
enhancement of 11.58% associated with a 'AAA' rating, and therefore to achieve 
the highest rating of 'AAA'. As a result, the rating benefits from the full 
potential uplift of seven notches with two unused notches. 
 
OUTLOOK 
The stable outlook reflects our view that adverse changes in the long-term ICR 
on Danske Bank or an increase in ALMM risk would not automatically result in a 
change to the ratings on the covered bonds. The program is eligible for seven 
notches of uplift from the ICR, but currently only makes use of five notches 
to attain a 'AAA' rating. Therefore, we could only lower the ratings on the 
covered bonds if we were to lower the long-term ICR on Danske Bank by more 
than two notches, or if the ALMM risk were to increase in combination with us 
lowering the long-term ICR on Danske Bank.

HD These European stocks are for the cautious contrarian; European banks have gotten a bad name, but thats about to change
BY Michael Brush
WC 1017 words
PD 27 August 2014
ET 11:27
SN MarketWatch
SC MRKWC
LA English
CY Copyright 2014 MarketWatch, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

European banks have gotten a bad name, but thats about to change

U.S. bank stocks have performed well in August, driven by improved loan growth and quality.

TD 

For the real contrarian bargains, though, bank investors should look to Europe, not the U.S. Sure, things appear bleak for those banks. But thats what makes them bargains with many of them trading well below valuations especially considering that things are about to get better for European banks.

Six to consider buying now are: Barclays, BNP Paribas, UniCredit, HSBC Holdings, ING Groep and Danske Bank.

Theres value globally in financials, but the European banks are once again the contrarian trade and they offer the best upside, in my view, says David Hussey, who oversees European investments in the London offices of Manulife Asset Management.

Heres whats troubling this group, and why these problems may not be such a big deal.

Regulatory crackdown

U.S. regulators continue to investigate and hit European banks with hefty fines for wrongdoing like interest rate and currency manipulation, assisting in tax evasion and violating economic sanctions.

This has frightened investors, but these big fines are one-off events. That makes the related stock weakness a nice opportunity for the cautious contrarian, Hussey says.

Russia-Ukraine tensions

Im not smart enough to know when or how this will resolve. But it will someday, removing this discount from European bank stocks. Remember Russias invasion of Crimea? Is that still a problem?

Sluggish European growth

Economic weakness has investors worried that Europe will slip back into recession. But economist Ed Yardeni, of Yardeni Research, doesnt think that will happen because European Central Bank President Mario Draghi is committed to reviving economic growth whatever it takes, as he famously said. Draghi has pledged to keep interest rates near zero for as long as necessary. He favors a weak euro to help European exports. And hes ready to use more unconventional instruments, Yardeni notes.

Free lunch for European banks

One of these is an easy-money bank funding program called targeted longer-term refinancing operations. This is an economic revival plan that will help banks in particular, by pushing capital into banks to get them to lend, says Chad Deakins, portfolio manager of the RidgeWorth International Equity Fund (STITX, US). It is a free lunch for European banks, he says. They can borrow at 0.25% as long as they loan it and grow their loan books.

Catalysts on the horizon

Besides these policy measures, European banks will be helped by the following catalysts.

* An ECB-led asset quality review of bank balance sheets released over the next few months should bolster confidence in bank capital strength.

* Banks that dont make the cut will merge with stronger ones, bringing about a consolidation wave that will shore up confidence in the sector and improve pricing by reducing competition.

* Most of the banks listed below already pay a decent dividend. But as European economies improve, banks will up their dividends. These banks are going to be hugely important yield stocks in the future, Hussey says. For example, he thinks Barclays could pay a 5% yield next year.

Now lets take a look at six attractive European banks in the current weakness.

Barclays

A blue chip U.K. bank, Barclays (BCS, US) has been caught up in investigations into whether it rigged key interest rates. This, plus the general litany of worries, has spooked investors, so the stock trades at 0.63 times book value.

But return on equity and capital strength are improving. Expect more, as the bank continues to restructure by disposing of riskier assets, cutting costs, and trimming its investment-banking division, Hussey says.

BNP Paribas

BNP Paribas (BNPQY, US) pleaded guilty recently to violating U.S. trade sanctions by helping companies do business in Sudan, Cuba and Iran. This, plus concerns about growth and Russia have the banks shares trading at just 0.72 times book value, and a price earnings to growth (PEG) ratio of 0.4 (anything under 1 is considered cheap). BNP Paribas has a solid balance sheet. And the shares will rebound as the bank benefits from restructuring and improvements in the European economy, Hussey argues.

UniCredit

Based in Italy, UniCredit (UNCFF, US) trades for just 0.68 times book value. But that will improve as this well-funded bank sees recovery in end markets like Italy, Germany and Eastern Europe, Hussey says. This is perhaps one of the best plays long-term for the recovery across Europe, he says.

HSBC Holdings

More globally diversified than many major European banks, with lots of exposure to Asia, HSBC (HSBC, US) is pricier than most big Europe-based banks, trading at about 1 times book value. Asian growth, cost cutting, and better returns on its generous capital cushion as interest rates rise should propel outperformance, Hussey says. The bank pays a nice 4.6% dividend yield.

ING Groep

Based in Amsterdam, ING (ING, US) has been boosting profits by cutting costs, trimming loan loss provisions and selling off non-bank assets like insurance. Despite the improvements, the stock still trades cheaply, at about 0.8 times earnings, says Jim Kee, president and chief economist for South Texas Money Management. It is one of the healthier banks, but the market has not yet priced a lot of improvement, he says.

Danske Bank

Based in Denmark and with big exposure to Ireland, Danske Bank (DNSKY, US) trades at about book value. But given the potential for loan growth and the 4% dividend yield, its stock looks too cheap and it should do nicely as sentiment toward Europe and European banks improve, says Deakins of the RidgeWorth International Equity Fund.

Disclosure: At the time of publication, Michael Brush owned none of the stocks mentioned in this column. The writer publishes the stock newsletter Brush Up on Stocks[http://www.uponstocks.com/].

HD Danske's pension unit cuts funds managed by Danske Capital
WC 249 words
PD 5 September 2014
ET 09:07
SN Reuters News
SC LBA
LA English
CY Copyright 2014 Thomson Reuters. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

COPENHAGEN, Sept 5 (Reuters) - Danske Bank's pension fund unit Danica Pension will cut funds invested through the group's asset management unit Danske Capital due to its plan to increase direct investments in unlisted companies, a spokesman of the fund said.

Spokesman Mikkel Bro Petersen told Reuters the fund believes that best results of investing in unlisted companies would be reached by managing them by itself, and not by outside asset managers.

TD 

"Our new investment strategy implies that some of the tasks earlier with Danske Capital will in the future be handled by Danica, because we assess it will give the best results for our customers," he said on Friday.

He would not say how much of Danica's total assets of 344 billion Danish crowns ($60 billion) are currently managed by Danske Capital. He also declined to say how much funds the unit is planning to transfer.

Danica, Denmark's second-largest commercial pension fund, made its first direct investment into companies in June this year, when it invested 400 million crowns in Danish shipper Unifeeder.

Danica's chief financial officer Jacob Aarup-Andersen told Reuters earlier the fund aims to inject 10-15 billion crowns into direct investments in the next two years, and possibly another 10 billion, if they continue to see possibilities. (1 US dollar = 5.7464 Danish crown) (Reporting by Teis Jensen; Editing by Jussi Rosendahl)

HD ECB to Share Partial Stress-Test Results With Banks; Analysts Expect Discussion Will Yield Positive News For Most Lenders
BY By Max Colchester and Gabriele Steinhauser
WC 856 words
PD 9 September 2014
ET 08:44
SN The Wall Street Journal Online
SC WSJO
ED WSJ.com
LA English
CY Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

LP 

LONDONEuropean Central Bank officials in coming weeks will meet with bank executives to share partial results of how their institutions fared in "stress tests," according to people familiar with the matter, a process that analysts expect will yield positive news for most lenders.

"Most of the heavy lifting has been done; the process has played out for some time now," said Kinner Lakhani, a banking analyst at Citigroup Inc. "A minority of banks may have to take additional measures."

TD 

The ECB has been scrutinizing the balance sheets of 120 eurozone banks, seeking to discover hidden risks and find out how they would survive another economic downturn.

The tests have two main goals: clean up the banks before the ECB starts supervising them in early November and persuade investors that, six years after the start of the global financial crisis, the European banking system is finally on a sound footing and can contribute to the Continent's recovery.

By sharing preliminary and partial test results with the banks, the ECB is giving them an opportunity to plug capital holes or point out miscalculations before the full results are made public at the end of October. Although the tests evaluate the banks' 2013 numbers, they will include a summary of capital raised until Sept. 30 this year.

That allows the ECB to achieve two somewhat contradictory goals: It needs some banks to fail the stress tests to make them more credible than previous rounds, but also doesn't want to spook markets by revealing major unsolved problems at big lenders.

Smaller banks in countries such as Italy, Germany, Austria and Greece are seen as most at risk of flunking the tests. But few analysts and investors expect big shocks, thanks to the industry's flurry of capital raising and shedding of assets over the past year.

Goldman Sachs analysts surveyed 125 institutional investors, and their consensus is that nine banks will fail, triggering a total of 51 billion ($65.8 billion) of capital increases. The banks seen as most likely to have shortfalls are Italy's Banca Monte dei Paschi di Siena SpA, Germany's Commerzbank AG and Banco Comercial Portugus SA, according to the survey.

Spokesmen for the Italian and Portuguese banks declined to comment. A Commerzbank spokesman pointed to remarks last month by finance chief Stephan Engels that "we still feel well positioned to pass the test."

It is not just eurozone banks that are at risk of failing the stress tests. About two dozen big European banks outside the currency bloc are undergoing the same exercise. A stress-test simulation of 34 banks run by analysts at Mediobanca Securities concluded that seven banks could fail. These included two British banks, Royal Bank of Scotland Group PLC and Lloyds Banking Group PLC, and two Nordic lenders, Skandinaviska Enskilda Banken AB and Danske Bank A/S.

RBS declined to comment. A Danske Bank spokesman said the bank believes it is well capitalized.

A spokesman for Lloyds said the Mediobanca study had flaws, including the use of different capital definitions in different countries. An SEB spokeswoman declined to comment.

European supervisors have worked closely with national regulators and bank executives to spur pre-emptive action by banks. This year, eurozone banks raised about 36.9 billion in common equity, according to SNL Financial. They also ditched soured loans and extraneous business units. European Union banks are on track to shed 100 billion of assets this year, according to PricewaterhouseCoopers.

European authorities hope this year's tests will be more credible than in the past. European stress tests in 2010 and 2011 were widely ridiculed for being overly soft. Banks such as Belgium's Dexia SA were given clean bills of health, only to collapse months later.

In those tests, European authorities allowed banks to plug their balance sheet numbers directly into stress-test models. Part of the problem was that the banks' numbers were sometimes wildly inaccurate.

This time, the ECB is working with national regulators to conduct its own analysis of banks' balance sheets.

The asset-quality review, which is being wrapped up, seeks to ensure that the numbers used for the stress test include a more-realistic valuation of banks' holdings.

The stress test itself will evaluate banks' 2013 numbers and subject them to hypothetical scenarios under which the EU suffers a deep economic downturn. Banks unable to withstand the shock have to raise capital or sell assets.

To prepare, banks will be given the full results 48 hours before they are made public, according a person familiar with the matter.

Authorities want the test to be tough, "but you don't want to create panic in the market" by having lots of banks fail, says lvaro Benzo, a partner at PricewaterhouseCoopers.

Analysts view the pre-emptive action taken by the banks as a sign that the test is being taken seriously.

Of the investors polled by Goldman Sachs, 89% said they expected the stress test to be credible.

HD *Fitch Affirms Realkredit Capital Centre T at 'AA+'; Outlook Stable
WC 1002 words
PD 17 September 2014
ET 13:11
SN Dow Jones Institutional News
SC DJDN
LA English
CY Copyright 2014, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

LP 

17 Sep 2014 13:11 ET Press Release: Fitch Affirms Realkredit Capital Centre T at 'AA+'; Outlook Stable

 
 
The following is a press release from Fitch Ratings:

TD 

Fitch Ratings-London-17 September 2014: Fitch Ratings has affirmed Realkredit Danmark's (Realkredit) Capital Centre T covered bonds at 'AA+' with a Stable Outlook. The rating action follows the issuance of covered bonds incorporating the changes in the Danish legislation earlier this year.

The amended Danish covered bond legislation applies a mandatory maturity extension to the covered bonds issued after March 2014, which have a shorter maturity than underlying loans.

KEY RATING DRIVERS

- The rating is based on Realkredit's Long-term Issuer Default Rating (IDR) of 'A', a Discontinuity Cap (D-Cap) of 2 (high risk) and the 11.5% overcollateralisation (OC), which provides more protection than the 11% breakeven OC for the rating.

The new Danish legislation introduces mandatory maturity extensions for Danish mortgage bonds that are secured by mortgage loans with longer maturities than the bonds. The extension can be triggered upon two events: i) if a refinancing auction fails for all bonds or ii) if the interest on the mortgage bonds rises by more than 5 percentage points within a year (only for bonds with an original maturity of up to 2 years). Between March 2014 and January 2015, the changes only apply to one-year bonds, after which they will apply to bonds with longer maturities as well.

The maturity extension addresses the reliance of the issuer to refinance on highly concentrated refinancing dates in order to repay the covered bonds. In Fitch's analysis, this liquidity risk is captured by restricting the liquidity gap and systemic risk component of the D-Cap to 2 (high risk), which is also the weakest link for Realkredit's Capital Centre T D-Cap.

Until all bonds issued prior to April 2014 have matured, the Capital Centre T will contain bonds with and without the extendible feature. Investors would have to rely heavily on the alternative manager to allocate the available funds should the issuer default, as this is not clearly outlined in the legislation for this interim period. Fitch expects that by 2017 less than 10% of the bonds in Capital Centre T will not have the extension feature.

Once the bonds have the extension feature, then upon the trigger of maturity extensions, the impacted covered bonds would effectively become pass-through bonds, mitigating the need to sell assets to meet the principal due at the bonds at their maturity dates. Fitch will reassess the liquidity gap and systemic risk D-Cap component once the majority of the bonds within the capital centre have the extension feature.

RATING SENSITIVITIES

- The covered bonds' rating would be vulnerable to a downgrade if any of the following occurred: (i) Realkredit's IDR was downgraded by one notch or more; or (ii) the D-Cap fell by one category or more; or (iii) the OC that Fitch takes into account in its analysis fell below 11%.
